Al-Mumana'a: Two Types and Its Primacy in Qiyas Debates
Elaborating on the first objection: prevention (mumana'a) and its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prevention of the description (man' al-wasf) and prevention of the ruling (man' al-hukm) and apply the proper sequence of objections in a qiyas debate using the example from Zakat al-Fitr.

Core Idea
Mumana'a denies the cause or the ruling; it is the first and necessary objection before all others.

This lesson delves into the first objection, al-mumana'a, exploring its two types and its role as the primary objection in qiyas. The passage provides a clear example from Zakat al-Fitr to illustrate how prevention of the description works. Understanding this distinction is crucial for both attacking and defending analogical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types of Mumana'a
From the passage, note that mumana'a is of two types: prevention of the description (man' al-wasf) and prevention of the ruling (man' al-hukm). Man' al-wasf denies that the claimed 'illah exists in the original or branch case. Man' al-hukm denies that the ruling (hukm) exists in the original case.
The 'description' (wasf) is the attribute claimed to be the cause.
2
Analyze the Zakat al-Fitr Example
The example: The Shafi'i claims that Zakat al-Fitr is obligated by the day of Fitr, so it does not fall by death before paying. The Hanafi responds: 'We do not accept its obligation by the day; rather, it becomes obligatory by the person (the wealthy individual).' This is a prevention of the description: the Hanafi denies that the day is the cause (wasf); instead, it is the person (or responsibility).
The example shows how mumana'a works in practice.
3
Understand the Procedural Primacy
The text states that mumana'a is the foundation of debate. The opponent (sail) must start with denial. Only if the proponent's cause is established should the opponent move to other objections like al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illah, al-qalb, etc.
This sequence ensures that the foundational elements are settled first.
4
Recognize the Basis (Sanad)
The hashia mentions that prevention can be with or without a basis (sanad). If with a basis, the denial is supported by evidence. Without basis, it is a bare denial, which may be weaker.
A sanad strengthens the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الأسولة المتوجهة على القياس ثمانية : الممانعة والقول بموجب العلة والقلب والعكس وفساد الوضع والفرق والنقض والمعارضة، أما الممانعة فنوعان: أحدهما منع الوصف والثاني منع الحكم، ومثاله في قولهم: صدقة الفطر وجبت بالفطر فلا تسقط بموته ليلة الفطر، قلنا: لا نسلم وجوبها بالفطر بل عندنا تجب المكلف. (۱) قوله: [الممانعة] هي أساس المناظرة وأصلها؛ لأن المناظرة وضعت على مثال الخصومات في الدعاوي الواقعة في حقوق العبادة، فالمعلّل يدعي لزوم الحكم الذي رام قصد إثباته على السائل، والسائل يدعي عليه فكان سبيله الإنكار كما أن سبيل المدعى عليه في الحقوق الدفع عن نفسه والإنكار فلا ينبغي له أن يتجاوز إلى غير الممانعة إلا عند الضرورة وهي أنه إذا ثبت ما ادعاه المجيب مؤثراً في الحكم يتجاوز السائل عنها إلى القول بموجب العلة إن أمكنه ذلك بأن كان الوصف من جنس الحكم وإلا يشغل بالقلب ثم بالمعارضة، فإذا زال الكلام إلى المعارضة سهل الأمر على المجيب، كذا في 'الفصول'. (۲) قوله [والفرق] ولقائل أن يقول : إنه ذكر الفرق ولم يشرع في مثاله كما شرع في سائر الأسولة، قيل لما ذكر مثل الفرق في الفصل السابق على هذا الفصل لم يذكر هاهنا الاختصار على أنه جاز أن يكون سهوا من الكاتب في نسخة المتن وهو غير بعيد، كذا في 'معدن الأصول'. (۳) قوله: [منع الوصف] بأن يقول: لا نسلم أنّ الوصف الذي جعله المعلّل علة موجود في المتنازع فيه والمنع إما مع السند أو بدونه والسند ما يكون المنع مبنياً عليه. (٤) قوله: [يمونه ويلي عليه] أي يقوم المكلّف بكفايته يحتمل مؤنة وقوله: «يلي عليه» أي: ذلك المكلف على ذلك الرأس لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «أدوا عمن تمونون أي تحملوا هذه المؤنة عمن و عليكم مؤنه، فعلم به أنّ الرأس سبب وسيأتي تحقيقه في باب الأسباب من هذا الكتاب. (٥) قوله : [ وكذلك] أي مثل الممانعة في الوصف في المسألة السابقة الممانعة فيما إذا قال: قدر الزكاة وهو خمسة دراهم واجب في الذمة لا تعلق لها بالعين كذا في 'المعدن'. (٦) قوله: [فلا يسقط بهلاك النصاب ... إلخ] جعل الشافعي رحمه الله وجوب مقدار الزكاه علته للحكم
Chapter on the objections directed against qiyas. There are eight objections: prevention, saying by the implication of the cause, reversal, inversion, corruption of placement, distinction, refutation, and counterargument. As for prevention, it is of two types: one is prevention of the description, and the second is prevention of the ruling. An example is in their saying: Zakat al-Fitr becomes obligatory by the evening of Fitr, so it is not void by his death on the night of Fitr. We say: We do not accept its obligation by the evening; rather, it becomes obligatory by the person. (1) His saying: [prevention] is the foundation of debate and its origin; because debate is modeled on litigation in claims concerning rights of worship. The proponent claims the bindingness of the ruling he intends to prove upon the opponent, and the opponent claims against him, so his way is denial, just as the way of the defendant in rights is to defend himself and deny. So he should not go beyond prevention except out of necessity, which is that if the claim of the respondent is established as effective in the ruling, the opponent goes beyond it to saying by the implication of the cause if he can, i.e., if the description is of the same genus as the ruling, otherwise he engages in reversal, then in counterargument. When the discussion shifts to counterargument, it becomes easy for the respondent. Thus in 'al-Fusul'. (2) His saying: [and distinction] And someone may say: He mentioned distinction but did not begin to give its example as he began in other objections. It is said: Because the example of distinction was mentioned in the previous chapter before this chapter, he did not mention it here for brevity, although it is possible that it was a scribal error in the copy of the text, and that is not unlikely. Thus in 'Ma'din al-Usul'. (3) His saying: [prevention of the description] is that he says: We do not accept that the description which the proponent made a cause exists in the disputed matter, and the prevention is either with a basis or without, and the basis is that on which the prevention is built. (4) His saying: [you support and are responsible for them] means the person responsible for his maintenance bears his expenses. And his saying: 'are responsible for them' means that person is in charge of that dependant, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Pay on behalf of those you support' i.e., you bear this expense for those whose expenses are upon you. So it is known by this that the dependant is a cause, and its verification will come in the chapter of causes from this book. (5) His saying: [and similarly] meaning like the prevention in the description in the previous issue, the prevention when he says: The amount of zakat which is five dirhams is obligatory in the liability, not tied to the property. Thus in 'al-Ma'din'. (6) His saying: [so it is not void by the destruction of the nisab... etc.] Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) made the obligation of the amount of zakat a cause for the ruling...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage from the hashia introduces the eight standard objections (as'ila) that can be raised against a qiyas argument in Hanafi usul. The objections are: al-mumana'a (prevention), al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illah (saying by the implication of the cause), al-qalb (reversal), al-'aks (inversion), fasad al-wad' (corruption of placement), al-farq (distinction), al-naqd (refutation), and al-mu'arada (counterargument). The focus here is on the first: mumana'a. It is divided into two types: prevention of the description (man' al-wasf) i.e., denying that the claimed 'illah actually exists in the original or branch case; and prevention of the ruling (man' al-hukm) i.e., denying the existence of the ruling in the original case. The text provides an example related to Zakat al-Fitr: the Shafi'i argues that Zakat al-Fitr is obligated by the arrival of the day of Fitr, so it does not fall by death before paying. The Hanafi responds by denying that the 'illah is the day; rather, it is the person (wealthy, free, resident). This is a prevention of the description. The hashia also explains that mumana'a is the foundational move in debate, analogous to a defendant denying a claim. It should be used first; only if the respondent's 'illah is established should the opponent move to other objections. The passage also clarifies that examples for 'farq' were omitted for brevity or possibly due to scribal error.

Key Terms
الممانعة (al-mumana'a): Prevention: an objection in qiyas where the opponent denies either the existence of the 'illah in the original case (man' al-wasf) or the existence of the ruling in the original case (man' al-hukm).
منع الوصف (man' al-wasf): Prevention of the description: denying that the attribute claimed as the 'illah exists in the original or branch case.
منع الحكم (man' al-hukm): Prevention of the ruling: denying that the ruling (hukm) exists in the original case (asl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Zakat al-Fitr example, which type of mumana'a does the Hanafi opponent use?
A) Prevention of the ruling (man' al-hukm)
B) Prevention of the description (man' al-wasf)
C) Reversal (al-qalb)
D) Counterargument (al-mu'arada)

Key Takeaway
Al-mumana'a is the first objection in qiyas debate, divided into man' al-wasf and man' al-hukm, and must be exhausted before other objections are raised.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
beginner
6 min
Al-Mumānaʿah: The First Objection to Qiyas
Defines the first objection to qiyas and its two subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subtypes of al-mumānaʿah (manʿ al-waṣf and manʿ al-ḥukm) and apply them to hypothetical qiyas arguments.

Core Idea
Al-mumānaʿah denies the analogy by challenging either the effective cause or the original ruling.

Al-mumānaʿah is the first of eight classical objections against qiyas in Hanafi usul. It is a precise tool: the opponent does not merely reject the analogy but denies either the description (al-waṣf) or the ruling (al-ḥukm) that the proponent relies on. This lesson unpacks the two subtypes with concrete examples, showing how this objection functions in juristic debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas argument
Look at the opponent's analogy: they claim that because the original case has a certain ruling due to a description, the branch case should share that ruling. Note the claimed effective cause (ʿillah) and the ruling in the origin.
This is the target of the objection.
2
Determine the subtype of mumānaʿah
If you deny that the alleged description exists in the contested case, it is manʿ al-waṣf. If you deny that the ruling in the origin is as claimed, it is manʿ al-ḥukm.
The objection must be specific; general denial is not a valid mumānaʿah.
3
Apply the objection to the given examples
Work through the three examples: zakat al-fiṭr, zakat on nisab, and wiping in wudu. For each, state whether the objection is manʿ al-waṣf or manʿ al-ḥukm and explain why.
This solidifies the distinction.
4
Formulate your own mumānaʿah
Given a new qiyas argument, construct a valid mumānaʿah objection by denying either the description or the ruling. Ensure precision.
Practice is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الأسولة المتوجهة على القياس ثمانية: الممانعة والقول بموجب العلة والقلب والعكس وفساد الوضع والفرق والنقض والمعارضة. أما الممانعة فنوعان: أحدهما منع الوصف والثاني منع الحكم. ومثاله في قولهم: صدقة الفطر وجبت بالفطر فلا تسقط بموته ليلة الفطر، قلنا: لا نسلم وجوبها بالفطر بل عندنا تجب بوجود المكلف. وكذلك إذا قيل: قدر الزكاة واجب في الذمة فلا يسقط بهلاك النصاب كالدين، قلنا: لا نسلم أن قدر الزكاة واجب في الذمة بل أداؤه واجب. ولئن قال: الواجب أداؤه فلا يسقط بالهلاك كالدين بعد المطالبة، قلنا لا نسلّم أنّ الأداء واجب في صورة الدين بل حرم المنع حتى يخرج عن العهدة بالتخلية. وهذا من قبيل منع الحكم. وكذلك إذا قال: المسح ركن في باب الوضوء فليسن تثليثه كالغسل، قلنا: لا نسلّم أن التثليث مسنون في الغسل فمنعنا الحكم.
Section: The objections directed against analogy are eight: prevention, necessity of the effect of the cause, inversion, reversal, corruption of position, differentiation, contradiction, and opposition. As for prevention, it is of two types: one is prevention of the description, and the second is prevention of the ruling. An example of it is in their saying: Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory due to the breaking of the fast, so it is not annulled by death on the night of the breaking of the fast. We said: We do not accept its obligation due to the breaking of the fast; rather, according to us, it is obligatory due to the existence of the responsible person. Likewise, when it is said: The amount of Zakat is an obligation on the liability (dhimmah) and is not annulled by the destruction of the nisab like a debt. We said: We do not accept that the amount of Zakat is an obligation on the liability; rather, its payment is obligatory. And if he says: The payment is obligatory, so it is not annulled by destruction like a debt after demand. We said: We do not accept that payment is obligatory in the case of debt; rather, prohibition of withholding (ḥurm al-manʿ) until release from liability by relinquishing (takhliyah) is what applies. This is a case of prevention of the ruling. Likewise, when he says: Wiping (al-masḥ) is a pillar in the chapter of ablution, so its threefold repetition should be considered sunnah like washing. We said: We do not accept that threefold repetition is sunnah in washing, so we prevent the ruling.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage introduces the first of eight standard objections used in Hanafi usul against qiyas: al-mumānaʿah (prevention). The objection is that the opponent denies either the existence of the alleged effective cause (ʿillah) in the contested case (manʿ al-waṣf) or denies the ruling in the original case (manʿ al-ḥukm). Three examples illustrate these subtypes: (1) In zakat al-fitr, the opponent claims the cause is the day of fitr; we respond that the cause is the existence of a liable person—this is prevention of the description. (2) In zakat, the opponent claims the amount is a liability debt-like; we respond that only payment is obligatory, not the amount itself—this is prevention of the ruling. (3) In wiping the head, the opponent claims that because washing three times is sunnah due to its being a pillar, wiping should also be done thrice; we respond that threefold repetition is not sunnah in washing, thus preventing the ruling in the original. The hashia (commentary) clarifies that mumānaʿah is the foundation of debate, and these structured denials are essential for sound juristic argumentation.

Key Terms
الممانعة (al-mumānaʿah): The first type of objection to qiyas, consisting of denying either the description or the ruling
منع الوصف (manʿ al-waṣf): Prevention of the description: denying that the alleged effective cause exists in the contested case
منع الحكم (manʿ al-ḥukm): Prevention of the ruling: denying that the ruling in the original case is as claimed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two subtypes of al-mumānaʿah (prevention) in objections to qiyas?
A) Prevention of the cause (manʿ al-ʿillah) and prevention of the effect (manʿ al-maʿlūl)
B) Prevention of the description (manʿ al-waṣf) and prevention of the ruling (manʿ al-ḥukm)
C) Prevention of the origin (manʿ al-aṣl) and prevention of the branch (manʿ al-farʿ)
D) Prevention of the analogy (manʿ al-qiyās) and prevention of the text (manʿ al-naṣṣ)

Key Takeaway
The first objection to qiyas, al-mumānaʿah, requires a specific denial of either the effective cause (manʿ al-waṣf) or the ruling in the origin (manʿ al-ḥukm).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Understanding Man' al-Hukm in Qiyas
Illustrates three examples of preventing the ruling in qiyas (man' al-hukm)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the method of man' al-hukm (prevention of the ruling) in Hanafi usul, using the examples of zakat, wiping, and currency exchange.

Core Idea
Man' al-hukm denies the ruling in the analogized-upon, breaking the qiyas.

In Hanafi usul, one of the key methods to refute an analogy (qiyas) is 'man' al-hukm' — preventing the ruling. Instead of attacking the effective cause ('illa), you deny that the ruling established in the original case (asl) actually applies to that case, or that it is as claimed. This lesson walks through the classic examples from Usool al-Shashi, illustrating how the Hanafi school uses this tool against Shafi'i analogies on zakat, wiping in wudu, and currency exchange. Understanding man' al-hukm sharpens your ability to evaluate analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed qiyas
For each example, first determine what the opponent (Shafi'i) is analogizing. What is the original case (asl) and what is the branch (far')? What ruling is being transferred?
E.g., Zakat is analogized to debt; wiping is analogized to washing of limbs.
2
Locate the ruling being prevented
In man' al-hukm, the Hanafi denies that the alleged ruling in the asl actually exists as described. What specific ruling is the opponent claiming is established in the asl?
E.g., 'Performance of debt is obligatory and does not fall by destruction.' The Hanafi denies this very ruling applies to zakat.
3
Understand the Hanafi reason for prevention
Why does the Hanafi deny that ruling? Usually it is based on a textual or rational distinction that changes the nature of the obligation. For zakat, it is tied to the wealth, not the dhimmah; for wiping, repetition is not the intended sunnah.
This step distinguishes man' al-hukm from man' al-wasf.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
What difference does this make in actual legal application? The prevention changes the outcome: zakat falls with destruction; wiping is done once comprehensively; currency exchange does not require physical hand-to-hand.
Knowing the thamarah helps you appreciate why the dispute matters.
5
Distinguish man' al-hukm from other objections
Note that some scholars consider the zakat example as prevention of the attribute (wasf), not the ruling. The commentary resolves this by stating that performance is itself a ruling. Understand the subtlety: sometimes the line between hukm and wasf can blur.
This meta-awareness is crucial for advanced usul analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

العلماء والفقهاء وحكاه الترمذي أيضاً عن الشافعي ومذهبه في التثليث حكاه ابـــــن المنذر عن أنس وسعيد بن جبير وعطاء وهو رواية عن أحمد وداود ومذهب الحنفية أنّ المسنون هو المرّة المستوعبة، قال ابن المنذر الأصح، كذا في "الحصول". ١٢ (٤) قوله: [قلنا] لا نسلّم أن التثليث مسنون في الغسل فمنعنا الحكم وهو سنّية التثليث في المقيس عليه وهو الغسل في الأعضاء الثلاثة، وبيانه أي بيان منع الحكم أنّ التكرار ليس Chapter of the Directed Questions. Example of prevention of the ruling (man' al-hukm). i.e., the amount of zakat. Does not fall. Rather its performance is obligatory. And if he said: what is obligatory to perform does not fall by destruction like a debt after... i.e., paying the debt to its owner by the debtor. About that he takes the amount of his debt from the property of his debtor. The demand. We said: We do not accept that performance is obligatory in the case of debt, rather prohibition of prevention is until it comes out for expense. i.e., liability of the debt. And if the creditor does not take the property. Another example of prevention of the ruling: in what was mentioned earlier, prevention of the ruling in the case when he said... about liability by relinquishing; this is of the type of prevention of the ruling. And likewise if he said: the wiping (al-mash). i.e., the wiping. i.e., wiping of the head. i.e., internal obligation. A pillar (rukn) in the chapter of ablution; then its tripling (tathleeth) is recommended like washing (ghusl). We said: We do not accept that tripling is recommended... and it is the obligation remaining after the destruction of the wealth. And we only prevent this cause ('illa) and say: We do not accept that the amount of zakat is an obligation in the liability (dhimmah); rather its performance is an obligation in the liability. Thus in 'Fusool al-Hawashi'. His statement [And this] i.e., our statement: We do not accept that the obligation of performance is established in the case of debt. His statement [of the type of prevention of the ruling] because the obligation of performance and its permissibility are among the rulings. And it could be said: The ruling in this qiyas is the non-falling of zakat due to destruction of wealth. As for the obligation of performance, it is made a comprehensive attribute (wasf jami') between the original (debt) and the branch (zakat); thus prevention of obligation is of the type of prevention of attribute, because it is an attribute of this ruling, not of the type of prevention of the ruling; so the example does not match the example. I say: The author (may Allah have mercy on him) only considered the obligation of performance as prevention of the ruling because performance is in the original among the rulings; so it does not harm that it is of the type of prevention of attribute that opposes the qiyas. Thus it is said. His statement [al-mash... etc.] the one who gives 'illa from the Shafi'is, for example, gives the cause for the ruling of the recommended nature of tripling of the wash on the washed limbs by the pillar nature: that the tripling in the obligatory wash was only due to the fact that washing is an obligation and a pillar for ablution, and the obligation is completed by recommended acts, and completion is only by repetition, and its perfection is by tripling. Then if this cause (i.e., pillar nature) is found in wiping, then its completion by tripling is also recommended. This is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) that it is recommended with different waters; he stated it in his books and a group of his followers are definite about it. But al-Rafi'i reported that being once is a view of our companions, and it is the madhhab of most scholars and jurists; al-Tirmidhi also reported it from al-Shafi'i; and his madhhab on tripling is reported by Ibn al-Mundhir from Anas, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, 'Ata', and it is a narration from Ahmad and Dawud. The Hanafi madhhab is that the recommended is the comprehensive once. Ibn al-Mundhir said: and who said it: Ibn 'Umar, Talha ibn Musarrif, al-Hakam, 'Imad, al-Nakha'i, Mujahid, Salim ibn 'Abdullah, al-Hasan al-Basri, Malik, Ahmad, al-Thawri, and others; Ibn al-Mundhir chose it. Ibn 'Adi said: All the narrators said wiping the head once, and it is the most correct. Thus in 'al-Hasool'. His statement [We said] We do not accept that tripling is recommended in washing; so we prevent the ruling, which is the recommendability of tripling in the analogized-upon (maqis 'alayh) i.e., washing in the three limbs. Its explanation, i.e., explanation of prevention of the ruling: repetition is not an intended sunnah in the original; because the attribute of pillar nature has no effect on repetition; its effect is only on the recommendability of completion; because supererogatory acts and obligations were only legislated as completers for the obligations; and because completion is the basis in all other pillars, and completion is only by lengthing the obligation in its place insofar as possible. Do you not see that standing, bowing, and prostration, their completion is by lengthening them, not by repeating them; likewise recitation. Except that we did not find a place for lengthening in washing; because when the obligatory covered its place, lengthening would be completion in other than the place of the obligation; so we necessarily resorted to repetition as a substitute for the original. And acting according to the original is possible in wiping the head; so we said: by lengthening in it through comprehensiveness. Understand. Thus in 'al-Fusool'.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from Usool al-Shashi with its hashia discusses the usul concept of 'man' al-hukm' (prevention of the ruling) in the context of qiyas. The author presents three examples where the Hanafi school prevents the ruling in the original case (asl), thereby defeating the analogy proposed by opponents. The first example concerns zakat: the Shafi'i analogizes zakat to debt, arguing that just as debt does not fall due to destruction of the debtor's wealth, zakat also does not fall if the wealth is destroyed before payment. The Hanafi response is not to accept that performance of zakat is obligatory in the liability (dhimmah) like debt; rather, zakat is linked to the wealth itself. The commentary clarifies that this is a prevention of the ruling (man' al-hukm) because the ruling in the original (debt) is that performance is obligatory and does not fall; the Hanafis deny this ruling in the context of zakat. A subtle point is made that some might consider this prevention of the attribute (wasf) rather than the ruling, but the author argues it is effectively prevention of the ruling since performance is itself a ruling. The second example involves wiping the head (masah) in wudu. The Shafi'i argues that since washing (ghusl) is a pillar (rukn) and it is recommended to wash three times, the same recommendation should apply to wiping the head if wiping is also a pillar. The Hanafi response is to deny that tripling is recommended in washing itself; rather, the recommended practice is to lengthen the wash over the entire limb, and repetition is only a substitute due to the inability to lengthen further. For the head, lengthening is possible through comprehensive wiping (istīʿāb), so repetition is not needed. Thus the ruling of recommending tripling is prevented. The third example (partially on page 264) concerns the condition of hand-to-hand exchange (taqabud) in ribawi transactions. The Shafi'i analogizes food sales to currency exchange, requiring immediate exchange in both. The Hanafi response is to deny that hand-to-hand exchange is a condition in currency exchange; rather, specification (ta'yin) of the currency is sufficient. This is another case of man' al-hukm: the ruling (condition of taqabud) in the original (currency exchange) is denied. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes that the Hanafi method is to analyze the effective cause ('illa) and either prevent the ruling in the asl or prevent the attribute, thereby refuting the qiyas.

Key Terms
منع الحكم (man' al-hukm): Prevention of the ruling in qiyas; denying that the ruling established in the original case (asl) applies to the branch (far'), thereby breaking the analogy.
التثليث (al-tathleeth): Performing an action three times, specifically in wudu and ghusl.
التقابض (al-taqabud): Hand-to-hand exchange; physical transfer of both countervalues in a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of man' al-hukm, what ruling do the Hanafis prevent concerning zakat compared to debt?
A) That zakat is obligatory like debt.
B) That the obligation of zakat performance remains after destruction of wealth.
C) That zakat is a right of Allah and not a human debt.
D) That zakat can be paid from any wealth.

Key Takeaway
Man' al-hukm is a method to refute qiyas by denying the ruling in the original case, not by attacking the cause.

Qawl bi-Mujab al-Illa: Conceding the Cause, Disputing the Effect
Defining 'qawl bi-mujab al-illa' with the elbow example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'qawl bi-mujab al-illa', identify its components, and apply it to the example of the elbow in wudu to distinguish between conceding the cause and disputing the effect.

Core Idea
Qawl bi-mujab al-illa: accept the cause, change the effect.

In usul al-fiqh, disputation often involves challenging an opponent's cause ('illa). But a more subtle argument is to concede the cause but argue that it leads to a different ruling than claimed. This is 'qawl bi-mujab al-illa'. In this lesson, we examine this technique through a classic Hanafi example: the elbow in wudu. Understanding this concept sharpens your ability to analyse legal reasoning without conceding the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Break down the Arabic phrase 'al-qawl bi-mujab al-illa' into its components: qawl (statement), mujab (entailment/requirement), illa (cause). The technique means 'speaking according to the entailment of the cause' – you accept the cause but argue that its necessary consequence (ma'lul) is different from what the opponent claims.
The definition in the text: 'taslimu kawn al-wasf illatan wa bayanu anna ma'lulaha ghayru ma idda'ahu al-mu'allil'.
2
Distinguish between cause and effect
The opponent identifies a description as the 'illa (cause) for a ruling. In qawl bi-mujab al-illa, you concede that the description is indeed the 'illa, but you argue that its ma'lul (legal effect) is a different ruling than what the opponent claims. This is more refined than outright denying the 'illa.
Concession of the 'illa is the key; the dispute shifts to the ma'lul.
3
Parse the example structure
The example: The verse (5:6) says 'wash your hands to the elbows'. The Shafi'i argues that the elbow is a limit and limits are not included, so the elbow is not washed. The Hanafi responds by conceding that the elbow is a limit, but then argues that it is the limit of the dropped part (al-saqit), i.e., the upper arm beyond the wash area. Thus the ma'lul of 'being a limit' here is not 'not washed' but 'boundary of the unwashed part'.
The Hanafi does not deny the 'illa (limit); he redefines what the 'illa entails.
4
Apply the ghayah principle
The text explains that the ghayah (purpose clause) 'ila al-marafiq' is for isqat (dropping/exclusion). This means the limit is excluded from the action. The elbow is the boundary of the dropped part (the upper arm), not of the washed part. This linguistic principle supports the Hanafi interpretation.
Hanafi usul distinguishes ghayah li'l-isqat from ghayah li'l-idkhal.
5
Contrast with direct denial
Compare qawl bi-mujab al-illa with simply denying that the elbow is a limit. The latter would be weaker because it contradicts the apparent meaning. By conceding the cause, the Hanafi argument stays within the opponent's framework and is more persuasive.
This technique is a form of munazarah strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الأسولة المتوجهة فشرط التقابض كيلا يكون بيع النسئة بالنسئة غير أن النقود لا تتعين إلا بالقبض عندنا. وأما القول بموجب العلة فهو تسليم كون الوصف علة وبيان أن معلولها غير ما ادعاه المعلل، ومثاله المرفق حد في باب الوضوء فلا يدخل تحت الغسل؛ لأن الحد لا يدخل في المحدود، قلنا: المرفق حد الساقط فلا يدخل تحت حكم الساقط؛ لأن الغاية هنا للإسقاط فكان المرفق حد الساقط، وكذا في كتب الأصول.
A section on the questions concerning condition of hand-to-hand exchange, so that it not be a sale of deferred for deferred, except that currencies are not specified except by possession according to us. And as for the argument by conceding the effective cause (qawl bi-mujab al-illa), it is to concede that the description is a cause and to show that its effect is other than what the claimer claimed. Its example: the elbow is a limit in the chapter of wudu, so it does not fall under washing, because the limit is not included in the limited. We said: the elbow is the limit of the dropped part (al-saqit), so it does not fall under the ruling of the dropped part, because the clause of purpose (ghayah) here is for dropping (isqat), and thus the elbow is the limit of the dropped part. And likewise in the books of usul.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Usul al-Shashi introduces the usul concept of 'qawl bi-mujab al-illa' (reasoning by conceding the cause). This is a technique in legal disputation where one accepts the opponent's identified cause ('illa) but argues that its legal effect (ma'lul) is not the ruling claimed by the opponent, but rather a different ruling. The text provides the classic example from fiqh al-wudu: the elbow (mirfaq) is a limit (hadd) in the verse on wudu (Quran 5:6: 'wash your faces and your hands to the elbows'). If someone argues that the elbow is not included in the washing because it is a limit and limits are not included in the limited (mahrud), the Hanafi response is to concede that the elbow is a limit, but then argue that it is the limit of the 'dropped part' (al-saqit), i.e., the part beyond the washing area, not the limit of the washed part. The word 'ila' (to) in the verse indicates that the elbow is the endpoint of what is washed, but the endpoint itself is not washed. The text clarifies that the ghayah (purpose clause) here is for isqat (dropping/exception), meaning that the limit excludes itself from the ruling. Thus, the elbow does not fall under the ruling of washing, but it also does not fall under the ruling of the dropped part because it is the limit of that dropped part. This argument demonstrates that the opponent's claimed ruling (that the elbow is not washed) is conceded, but the cause (being a limit) yields a different entailment (that the elbow is the limit of the dropped part), thus countering the opponent's reasoning.

Key Terms
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa): Conceding that a certain description is the effective cause ('illa) but arguing that its legal effect (ma'lul) is different from what the opponent claims.
الغاية للإسقاط (al-ghāyah li’l-isqāt): A purpose clause (ghayah) that indicates dropping (isqat), meaning the limit is excluded from the action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the elbow in wudu, what does the Hanafi argument 'al-mirfaqu haddu al-saqit' mean?
A) The elbow is washed just like the rest of the hand.
B) The elbow is the limit of the part that is dropped (the upper arm), so it is not included in the wash.
C) The elbow is included in the wash because the limit is inside the limited.
D) The elbow is not washed because it is the limit of the washed part.

Key Takeaway
Qawl bi-mujab al-illa is a powerful argument technique in Hanafi usul: accept the opponent's cause but show that it entails a different ruling.

The Definition of Qawl bi-Mujab al-illah and its Application: The Elbow in Wudu
Definition of a qiyas objection with a fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qawl bi-mujab al-illah, identify its structure as a qiyas objection, and apply it to the Hanafi ruling on washing the elbow in wudu.

Core Idea
Accepting the cause is not accepting the ruling — qawl bi-mujab al-illah disputes the claimed effect, not the cause.

This passage introduces a key objection in qiyas: the opponent concedes the cause (illah) but argues that the effect (ma'lul) is different from what the proponent asserts. The Hanafi example of the elbow in wudu illustrates how this objection is used to defend the ruling that the elbow must be washed. Understanding this concept sharpens your ability to critique analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the term
Break down the Arabic: qawl (statement), bi-mujab (according to the requisite), al-illah (the cause). The overall meaning: 'a statement according to the requisite of the cause'.
2
Identify the two components
First, the opponent acknowledges (taslim) that the description is a valid cause. Second, the opponent shows (bayan) that the effect claimed by the proponent is not the actual effect of that cause.
This distinguishes it from rejecting the cause (naqd) or proposing a counter-cause (mu'arada).
3
Analyze the example
The proponent says the elbow is a limit (hadd) and thus not washed. The Hanafi accepts it is a limit but retorts: it is the limit of the omitted part (saqit), not of the washed part. Therefore, its ruling is inclusion in washing.
Key distinction: the limit belongs to the thing limited – here the omitted part is the limit beyond the washed part, so the elbow is attached to the washed part.
4
Understand the logical move
The opponent's cause (being a limit) is accepted, but the effect (exclusion from washing) is shown to be false because the cause's actual effect is to define the boundary of omission, not the omission of the boundary itself.
This forces the proponent to refine the illah: maybe 'hadd' is not the correct cause, or the effect needs reinterpretation.
5
Recognize the broader usul principle
Qawl bi-mujab al-illah is a valid objection that checks the coherence of causal reasoning. It teaches that accepting the cause does not bind you to the opponent's conclusion.
This is a standard tool in Hanafi usul for evaluating qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمّا القول بموجب العلة فهو تسليم كون الوصف علة وبيان أن معلولها غير ما ادعاه المعلل، ومثاله المرفق حد في باب الوضوء فلا يدخل تحت الغسل؛ لأن الحد لا يدخل في المحدود، قلنا : المرفق حد الساقط فلا يدخل تحت حكم الساقط.
As for the statement according to the requisite of the cause, it is acknowledging that the description is a cause and showing that its effect is different from what the proponent of the cause claimed. Its example is the elbow being a limit in the chapter of wudu, so it does not enter under washing, because the limit is not included in the limited. We said: The elbow is the limit of the omitted part, so it does not enter under the ruling of the omitted part.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qawl bi-mujab al-illah' (statement according to the requisite of the cause), which is one of the types of objections (su'al) in qiyas. The opponent accepts the cause (illah) proposed by the proponent but argues that the effect (ma'lul) claimed by the proponent is not the actual effect; rather the cause leads to a different effect. The example given is the elbow in the verse on wudu (Quran 5:6). The proponent argues that the elbow is a limit (had) and limits are not included in the limited (mahdud), so the elbow is not washed. The Hanafi response (using qawl bi-mujab al-illah) is to accept that the elbow is a limit, but argue that it is the limit of the omitted part (saqit) — the part beyond the elbow that is not washed — so the elbow does not enter under the ruling of the omitted part, meaning it is not omitted; it is included in washing. This is a typical Hanafi position that the elbow is included in the washing of the arm. The commentary explains that the limit (ghayah) in the verse is for exclusion (isqat), so the elbow is the limit of the excluded part, not of the washed part.

Key Terms
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi-mujab al-illah): Acknowledging that the description is a cause but showing that its effect is different from what the proponent claims.
الحد (al-hadd): A limit or boundary that separates one thing from another, or the part that is at the extreme end.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the elbow, what does the Hanafi 'qawl bi-mujab al-illah' objection accept?
A) That the elbow is not a limit
B) That the elbow is a limit but it is the limit of the omitted part
C) That the elbow is a limit and therefore excluded
D) That the cause (being a limit) is invalid

Key Takeaway
Qawl bi-mujab al-illah is a precise usul tool that allows an opponent to accept the cause while challenging the claimed effect, forcing a more accurate causal analysis.

Types of Qalb (Reversal) in Qiyas
Definition of qalb and its two literal types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb, distinguish between its two types (turning upside-down and turning inside-out), and explain their metaphorical use in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Qalb (reversal) in usul is a twofold concept: flipping a bowl and turning a sack inside-out.

Qalb (reversal) is a technical term in Hanafi usul used as a type of objection (su'al) against a qiyas argument. This lesson unpacks its literal meaning as defined in Usul al-Shashi, providing the foundation for understanding how reversal is applied to invalidate analogies by flipping the relationship between cause and ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'qalb'
Locate the term in the source text and note its Arabic root (q-l-b), meaning to turn or reverse.
Qalb is distinct from naqd (invalidation).
2
List the two types from the text
From the passage, extract the two types: (1) making the upper part lower and vice versa (like flipping a bowl), (2) making the inner part outer and vice versa (like turning a sack inside-out).
These are literal, physical reversals.
3
Understand the analogy to usul
Relate each literal type to a method of objection in qiyas: the first type corresponds to reversing the relationship between asl and far', and the second to reversing between illah and ruling.
The commentary explains the usul application.
4
Distinguish qalb from other objections
Note that qalb is a specific form of naqd that involves reversal, not just pointing out a contradiction. Use the common misunderstanding: qalb vs naqd.
This distinction will be developed in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القلب فنوعان: أحدهما أن يجعل أسفل الشيء أعلاه وأعلاه أسفله كقلب القصعة والكوز، والثاني أن يجعل باطن الشيء ظاهره وظاهره باطنه كقلب الجراب.
As for reversal (qalb), it is of two types: one is to make the lower part of a thing its upper and its upper its lower, like flipping a bowl or a pitcher; the second is to make the inner of a thing its outer and its outer its inner, like turning a sack inside out.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines 'qalb' (reversal), which is a type of objection (su'al) in qiyas. It is a linguistic term, not a fiqh ruling. The first type is physical reversal of top and bottom, like flipping a bowl. The second is turning inside out, like a sack. In usul, qalb refers to reversing the relationship between the cause (illah) and the ruling, or between the original and the branch, to test the validity of the analogy. The two types mentioned here are literal meanings, but in usul, qalb is often used to invalidate a qiyas by showing that if the same cause is applied, the original would be affected similarly. This passage is from the commentary explaining the term before applying it to fiqh examples.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): Reversal: making something opposite to its original state, either by turning upside down or inside out.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what are the two types of qalb (reversal) mentioned?
A) Reversal of cause and effect
B) Turning upside down and turning inside out
C) Reversal of ruling and subject
D) Reversal of time and place

Key Takeaway
Qalb in usul refers to two literal types of reversal (upside-down and inside-out) that metaphorically apply to testing the validity of qiyas.

The Hanafi Response to Shafi'i on Ta'yin al-Niyya in Ramadan
Hanafi response to Shafi'i objection on ta'yin al-niyya in Ramadan fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument against requiring ta'yin al-niyya in Ramadan fast and contrast it with the Shafi'i position using the hadith.

Core Idea
When the Law itself specifies the act, the servant's explicit intention is not required.

The Shafi'i school argues that every obligatory fast requires explicit specification of intention (ta'yin al-niyya), just like make-up fasts (qada). The Hanafi school responds that the Law has already specified the Ramadan fast through the Prophet's hadith, making the servant's individual specification redundant. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i objection
Identify the Shafi'i claim: Ramadan fast is obligatory, so it requires ta'yin, analogous to make-up fasts.
The objection uses qiyas to qada.
2
Identify the Hanafi principle
Recognize that the Hanafi position accepts the need for specification in principle, but argues it is already provided by the Law.
3
Cite the hadith as dalil
The Prophet said: 'When Sha'ban passes, there is no fast except for Ramadan.' This hadith specifies the Ramadan fast as the only permissible fast after Sha'ban.
The hadith is a dalil naqli.
4
Explain why qiyas is overridden
The hadith provides a direct textual specification, making the analogy to make-up fasts invalid because make-up fasts lack such a specification.
Hanafi usul prioritizes textual dalil over qiyas.
5
Note the practical difference
In the Hanafi school, one may intend simply to fast in Ramadan without articulating 'Ramadan'; in the Shafi'i school, explicit intention of Ramadan is required.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الأسولة المتوجهة من جهة الشافعي في اشتراط تعيين النية في صوم رمضان. لأن الحد لا يدخل في المحدود، وكذلك يقال : صوم رمضان صوم فرض فلا يجوز بدون التعيين كالقضاء، قلنا: صوم الفرض لا يجوز بدون التعيين إلا أنه وجد التعيين هاهنا من جهة الشرع فإنه قال عليه السلام إذا انسلخ شعبان فلا صوم إلا عن رمضان. فلا يشترط تعيين العبد، وأما القلب فنوعان: أحدهما أن يجعل ما جعله المعلل علة للحكم معلولا لذلك الحكم، ومثاله في الشرعيات
Chapter on the objections from the side of al-Shafi'i regarding the requirement of specifying intention in the fast of Ramadan. Because the definition does not enter into the defined, and likewise it is said: The fast of Ramadan is an obligatory fast, so it is not permissible without specification like the make-up fast. We said: The obligatory fast is not permissible without specification, but specification is found here from the side of the Law, for he (peace be upon him) said: 'When Sha'ban passes, there is no fast except for Ramadan.' So the specification by the servant is not required. As for inversion, it is of two types: one is that what the mu'allil made the cause of the ruling is made the effect of that ruling, and its example in legal matters.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents a typical usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Shafi'i objection is that in Ramadan fast, the servant must specifically intend the Ramadan fast, just as in make-up fasts. The Hanafi response is that specification is already provided by the Law through the hadith 'When Sha'ban passes, there is no fast except for Ramadan,' so the servant's explicit intention is not required. The passage then transitions to discuss 'qalb' (inversion) in qiyas, a method to refute an opponent's analogy by reversing the cause and effect. The commentary expands on the hadith and the nature of intention. The Hanafi position relies on the principle that when the Law itself specifies the act, the servant's individual specification is unnecessary.

Key Terms
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyya): Specification of intention to distinguish the specific obligatory fast (e.g., Ramadan) from other fasts
قضاء (qada): Make-up fast for a missed obligatory fast

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because fasting in Ramadan is not obligatory
B) Because the Law itself has specified it via the hadith
C) Because intention is not required at all for fasting
D) Because the Shafi'i school accepts this ruling

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when the Law provides explicit specification, individual intention specification is not required.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

5 min
Qalb: Inversion as a Method of Refuting Qiyas
Definition of qalb as a method of refuting qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb and identify one type of qalb used to refute a qiyas argument.

Core Idea
Qalb inverts the cause and effect to undermine an opponent's analogy.

Qalb is a technique in usul al-fiqh used to rebut an opponent's qiyas by reversing their causal reasoning. The passage defines one type of qalb, where what the opponent made a cause (illa) is instead treated as the effect (ma'lul), thereby challenging the validity of their analogy. This lesson dissects the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qalb
The passage states: 'Qalb is of two types; one is that what the mu'allil made the cause of the ruling is made the effect of that ruling.'
Mu'allil refers to the person constructing the qiyas.
2
Understand the inversion process
In qiyas, the mu'allil asserts that A is the cause (illa) of ruling R in the original case, and B (in the new case) shares that cause, so R applies. In qalb, the opponent argues that instead, R is the cause of A, breaking the analogy.
This type of qalb challenges the illa by reversing its relationship with the ruling.
3
Consider an example (not in the text)
A common example: In the qiyas of khamr (wine) to qat' (date wine), some argue that khamr is prohibited because it intoxicates, so qat' should be prohibited. Using qalb, one could say that prohibition itself is the cause of intoxication (i.e., the ruling creates the attribute), which is absurd, thereby refuting the analogy.
This example is drawn from standard usul texts to illustrate qalb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الأسولة المتوجهة من جهة الشافعي في اشتراط تعيين النية في صوم رمضان. لأن الحد لا يدخل في المحدود، وكذلك يقال : صوم رمضان صوم فرض فلا يجوز بدون التعيين كالقضاء، قلنا: صوم الفرض لا يجوز بدون التعيين إلا أنه وجد التعيين هاهنا من جهة الشرع فإنه قال عليه السلام إذا انسلخ شعبان فلا صوم إلا عن رمضان. فلا يشترط تعيين العبد، وأما القلب فنوعان: أحدهما أن يجعل ما جعله المعلل علة للحكم معلولا لذلك الحكم، ومثاله في الشرعيات
Chapter on the objections from the side of al-Shafi'i regarding the requirement of specifying intention in the fast of Ramadan. Because the definition does not enter into the defined, and likewise it is said: The fast of Ramadan is an obligatory fast, so it is not permissible without specification like the make-up fast. We said: The obligatory fast is not permissible without specification, but specification is found here from the side of the Law, for he (peace be upon him) said: 'When Sha'ban passes, there is no fast except for Ramadan.' So the specification by the servant is not required. As for inversion, it is of two types: one is that what the mu'allil made the cause of the ruling is made the effect of that ruling, and its example in legal matters.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents a typical usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Shafi'i objection is that in Ramadan fast, the servant must specifically intend the Ramadan fast, just as in make-up fasts. The Hanafi response is that specification is already provided by the Law through the hadith 'When Sha'ban passes, there is no fast except for Ramadan,' so the servant's explicit intention is not required. The passage then transitions to discuss 'qalb' (inversion) in qiyas, a method to refute an opponent's analogy by reversing the cause and effect. The commentary expands on the hadith and the nature of intention. The Hanafi position relies on the principle that when the Law itself specifies the act, the servant's individual specification is unnecessary.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): Inversion; a method of refuting qiyas by making the cause the effect and vice versa

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Making the cause the effect of the ruling
B) Denying the existence of any cause
C) Proposing a new ruling
D) Accepting the opponent's qiyas

Key Takeaway
Qalb is a valid usul method to refute an opponent's qiyas by inverting the claimed cause-effect relationship.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Qalb (Inversion) in Qiyas: Definition and Types
Definition and two examples of qalb as a refutation method in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb and apply its two types to refute an opponent's qiyas by swapping cause and effect.

Core Idea
Qalb invalidates an analogy by swapping the cause and effect, provided the cause is a ruling.

In Hanafi usul, qalb is a powerful refutation technique used against an opponent's qiyas. Instead of rejecting the analogy outright, you reverse the causal relationship: what the opponent made the cause of the ruling, you treat as the effect, and vice versa. This lesson defines qalb and illustrates its two types with classical examples, including the riba analogy and the sanctity of the Haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mu'allil's claim
Determine what the opponent (mu'allil) claims as the cause (illa) and what the ruling (hukm) is in their analogy.
The mu'allil must present a cause that is itself a ruling, not a pure description.
2
Isolate the cause and effect
Separate the cause (what they say necessitates the ruling) from the effect (the ruling itself). In the first example, the Shafi'i mu'allil claimed that riba in a large quantity (cause) necessitates riba in a small quantity (effect).
3
Perform the inversion
Swap the cause and effect: treat the original cause as the effect and the original effect as the cause. In the example, you would say: 'No, rather the occurrence of riba in a small quantity necessitates its occurrence in a large quantity.'
The inversion must be logically parallel, just as currency works both ways.
4
Verify the condition
Ensure that the cause you are inverting is itself a ruling (hukm). If it is a pure description (wasf), qalb does not apply. This condition separates valid qalb from mere contradiction.
In the Haram example, the prohibition of taking life is a ruling, so it can be inverted to the prohibition of taking a limb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القلب فنوعان: أحدهما أن يجعل ما جعله المعلل علة للحكم معلولا لذلك الحكم، ومثاله في الشرعيات كقول الشافعية جريان الربا في الكثير يوجب جريانه في القليل كالأثمان فيحرم بيع الطعام بالحفنتين منه ، قلنا : لا بل جريان الربا في القليل يوجب جريانه في الكثير كالأثمان وكذلك في مسألة الملتجئ بالحرم حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمة إتلاف الطرف
As for inversion, it is of two types: one is that what the mu'allil made the cause of the ruling is made the effect of that ruling. Its example in legal matters is like the statement of the Shafi'is: The occurrence of riba in a large quantity necessitates its occurrence in a small quantity, like in the case of currency, so selling food with two handfuls of it is prohibited. We said: No, rather the occurrence of riba in a small quantity necessitates its occurrence in a large quantity, like in the case of currency. And similarly in the issue of the one seeking refuge in the sanctuary: the prohibition of taking life necessitates the prohibition of taking a limb.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines 'qalb' (inversion) as a method to refute an opponent's analogy. The mu'allil (the one making the analogy) claims a certain cause for a ruling; the opponent reverses it, making the cause the effect and vice versa. Two examples are given: (1) The Shafi'i claim that riba in large quantities implies riba in small quantities is inverted: instead, riba in small quantities implies it in large quantities, analogous to currency where both small and large amounts are ribawi. (2) In the case of a criminal seeking refuge in the Haram, the Shafi'i argue that the prohibition of taking life (greater) implies the prohibition of taking a limb (lesser); the Hanafi inversion would be the opposite. The commentary clarifies that qalb is valid only when the cause is itself a ruling; if the cause is a pure description, qalb does not apply. The passage sets up the definition and first type of qalb.

Key Terms
قلب (qalb): Reversing the cause and effect in the opponent's analogy to refute it
معلل (mu'allil): The one who makes the analogy, presenting a cause and effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of qalb given in the text, what is inverted?
A) The prohibition of selling food is swapped with the permission of selling currency.
B) The occurrence of riba in a large quantity is made the effect, and in a small quantity the cause.
C) The Shafi'i claim is rejected by denying the analogy entirely.
D) The cause (large quantity) is replaced with the effect (small quantity) without change.

Key Takeaway
Qalb (inversion) refutes an opponent's qiyas by swapping the cause and effect, provided the cause is itself a ruling (hukm).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Naqd and Mu'arada in Qiyas: Objections and Responses
Discussion of naqd and mu'arada in qiyas with examples from Islamic rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between naqd (refutation) and mu'arada (opposition) in Hanafi qiyas, and apply the Hanafi response of refining the 'illa to counter naqd.

Core Idea
Naqd exposes inconsistency in the 'illa; mu'arada provides a counter-'illa without invalidating the original evidence.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, we explore two critical objection techniques in analogical reasoning (qiyas): naqd and mu'arada. Through examples of Islam and apostasy, and wudu and niyyah, you will see how Hanafi usul scholars defend their positions by refining the effective cause ('illa) or distinguishing between types of purification.

Guided Path
1
Define naqd and mu'arada
Identify the Arabic terms and their definitions from the passage. Note that naqd involves existence of the 'illa without the ruling, while mu'arada involves a counter-'illa that prevents the ruling without invalidating the original evidence.
The passage explicitly states: 'الفرق بين التناقض والتعارض أن التناقض يوجب بطلان نفس الدليل والتعارض يمنع ثبوت الحكم من غير أن يتعرض الدليل'.
2
Examine the first naqd example: Islam and apostasy
Consider the claim that Islam should dissolve marriage like apostasy. Identify the claimed 'illa (being opposite to apostasy) and the Hanafi response: Islam is a protecting covenant, naqd applies because Islam exists without dissolution in many cases.
The Hanafi response uses the hadith 'الإسلام عهد عاصم للملك والحقوق'.
3
Examine the second naqd example: wudu and intention
Analyze the opponent's qiyas that wudu requires niyyah like tayammum because both are acts of worship. Apply naqd: the 'illa of purification exists in washing clothes but no niyyah required.
This forces the opponent to refine the 'illa, which the Hanafis do by distinguishing haqiqi (physical) from hukmi (legal) purification.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to naqd
The Hanafi response changes the 'illa from absolute purification to legal purification (tahara hukmiyya), which is non-rational and thus requires intention to be actualized. This shows how a mujtahid can refine the 'illa to avoid naqd.
This refinement is a key feature of Hanafi usul: responding to naqd by specifying the 'illa or identifying a preventer.
5
Distinguish naqd from mu'arada
Using the definitions, note that naqd invalidates the evidence (or at least the claimed 'illa), while mu'arada merely prevents the ruling by offering a competing 'illa. The passage gives examples of mu'arada in the context of wudu and wiping (masah).
Mu'arada does not destroy the original evidence; it creates a conflict that must be resolved through tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف في الدين. فإن ارتداد أحدهما يفسده. فصل الأسولة المتوجهة أي ملك النكاح. النكاح فيفسده كارتداد أحد الزوجين فإنه جعل الإسلام علة لزوال الملك، النكاح. أي عرف حافظا. أي الخصم. والحقوق. الإسلام. أي مثل فساد الوضع في مسئلة الإسلام فساد الوضع في إلخ. قلنا الإسلام عهد عاصما للملك فلا يكون مؤثرا في زوال الملك، وكذلك أي القدرة على نكاحها. أي نكاح الحرة. أي الحر. مثل عدم الجواز فيما إلخ. في مسئلة طول الحرة إنه حر قادر على النكاح فلا يجوز له الأمة كما لو كانت أي الناكح. أي الناكح. أي نكاح الأمة. الوصف المذكور. تحته حرة، قلنا: وصف كونه حرا قادرا يقتضي جواز النكاح فلا يكون مؤثرا أي نكاح الأمة. أي الوضوء. في عدم الجواز، وأما النقض فمثل ما يقال: الوضوء طهارة فيشترط له النية والجامع أن كل واحد منهما عبادة ولا عبادة بدون النية. عنها. كالتيمم، قلنا : ينقض بغسل الثوب والإناء، وأمّا المعارضة فمثل ما يقال: يشترط له النية. لوجود العلة أي الطهارة مع تخلف الحكم (أي اشتراط النية). الفرق بين التناقض والتعارض أن التناقض يوجب بطلان نفس الدليل والتعارض يمنع ثبوت الحكم من غير أن يتعرض الدليل. فيوجب فساد النكاح كالردّة، قلنا: هذا فاسد وضعاً؛ لأنه أي: المعلل جعل الإسلام علة لزوال الملك، والإسلام عهد عاصماً للملك والحقوق كما أيّ أسلم في دار الحرب فقد عصم نفسه وماله وولـــده الصغير، فلا يكون مؤثّراً في زوال الملك، كذا في "الفصول". قوله: [وأمّا النقض] فهو وجود العلة وتخلف الحكم عنه، سواء كان لمانع أو لغيره عند من لم يجوز التخصيص، أي: تخصيص العلّة، فالتخصيص مناقضة عندهم، وعند من جوزه هو تخلف الحكم عما ادعاه المعلّل علة لا لمانع، مثاله ما يقال : الوضوء طهارة، فيشترط فيه النية كالتيمم، والجامع أنّ كلا منهما عبادة، ولا عبادة بدون النية، قلنا: ينقض بغسل الثوب والإناء لوجود العلّة وهي الطهارة مع تخلف الحكم، وهو اشتراط النية، لأنه لم يشترط النية فيهما فتدبّر، كذا قيل. قوله: [قلنا ... فعاد المعلّل فارقاً بتغيير العلة إلى أنّ العلة ليست ما فهمتم حتى تقولوا: يلزم تخلّف الحكم عن علّته في طهارة الثوب، بل العلة عندنا هو الطهارة الحكميّة، وهي غير معقولة، فيحتاج فيها إلى النية، وأجاب عنه الحنفية : بأن تنجس البدن بخروج النجاسة أمر معقول، نعم! الاقتصار على الأعضاء الأربعة مع وجوب تطهير البدن كلّه غير معقول، قد جوّز لدفع الحرج، فإذا تنجس البدن والماء مطهر بطبيعة المطهر شرعاً أيضاً فيتطهر به ويزيل النجاسة بلا حاجة إلى النية بخلاف التيمم، فإنّ التراب ملوّث لا مطهر، قلت لي فيه نظر فإنّ هذا غفلة ظاهرة عن حقائق الأجناس الحقيقية، والحكميّة، فإنّ مقصوده أنّ الحكميّة مانعيّة شرعيّة غير محسوسة، ومعنى اعتباري غير معقول وجوده على البدن، حتى يطهّره والمطهّر والنجس يزيله الماء، فإنّ الماء إنّما المعقول من طبعه إزالة النجس المحسوس لا إزالة معنى عقلي اعتباري، فلا محالة يفتقر إلى النية حتى يتحقق به المعنى الحكمي الشرعي. فصل الأسولة المتوجهة في الوضوء. المسح ركن في الوضوء فليسن تثليثه كالغسل، قلنا: المسح ركن فلا يسن وهو وجود العلة مع تخلف الحكم عنها. تثلیثه كمسح الخف والتيمم. المأمور به في الشرع، كذا في "الحصول"، فإن قلت: كلامنا في الطهاره التي عبادة، وغسل الثوب والإناء ليس بعبادة، قلت: مثل غسل الثوب والإناء ليس الوضوء في نفسه بعبادة أيضاً، فإنّ العبادة فعل يأتى به المراد تعظيماً لله تعالى وتذلّلاً وخضوعاً، والوضوء في نفسه إراقة الماء، ولا يعقل فيه معنى العبادة، بل هو أهلية في المرء للعبادة :أي الصلاة، فاندفع ما أوردت.

Because of the existence of the 'illa, i.e., purification, with the lack of the ruling (i.e., requirement of intention). The difference between contradiction and opposition is that contradiction necessitates the invalidity of the evidence itself, while opposition prevents the establishment of the ruling without affecting the evidence. So it necessitates the invalidity of marriage like apostasy. He said: [As for naqd] it is the existence of the 'illa and the lack of the ruling from it, whether due to a preventer or otherwise for those who do not allow specification, i.e., specification of the 'illa, so specification is a contradiction for them, and for those who allow it, it is the lack of the ruling from what the attribute-giver claimed as 'illa not due to a preventer. Example: what is said: Ablution is purification, so intention is required in it like tayammum, and the common factor is that each of them is an act of worship, and no act of worship is valid without intention. We said: It is refuted by washing the garment and the vessel because of the existence of the 'illa, which is purification, with the lack of the ruling, which is the requirement of intention, because intention is not required in them, so reflect, as is said. He said: [We said...] then the attribute-giver responded by changing the 'illa to the effect that the 'illa is not what you understood so that you may say: the lack of the ruling from its 'illa follows in the purification of the garment, rather the 'illa according to us is the legal purification, which is not rationally comprehensible, so it needs intention. The Hanafis answered: that the soiling of the body by the exit of impurity is a rational matter, yes! Restriction to the four limbs despite the obligation to purify the entire body is not rational; it was allowed to avoid hardship. So when the body is soiled and water is a purifier by nature, and a purifier by law as well, then one purifies with it and removes the impurity without need for intention, unlike tayammum, for the earth is soiling, not purifying. I said: there is consideration in it, for this is a clear neglect of the realities of true and legal categories. For his intent is that the legal is a legal preventer not perceptible, and a conventional meaning not rationally conceivable as existing on the body such that it purifies it, while the purifier and the impurity are removed by water. For the only rationally conceivable quality of water is to remove perceptible impurity, not to remove a rational conventional meaning. So inevitably it depends on intention to realize the legal conventional meaning. Section of the directed questions on ablution. Wiping is a pillar in ablution, so its repetition three times is not sunnah like washing. We said: Wiping is a pillar, so its repetition three times is not sunnah, and it is existence of the 'illa with the lack of the ruling from it. Its repetition three times is like wiping over the khuff and tayammum. That which is commanded in law, as in 'al-Husul'. For if you say: our discussion is about purification which is an act of worship, while washing the garment and the vessel is not an act of worship. I say: Like washing the garment and the vessel, the ablution itself is also not an act of worship in itself. For worship is an action done for the purpose of magnifying Allah Almighty and humbling oneself and showing submissiveness, and ablution in itself is pouring water, and the meaning of worship is not rationally conceivable in it; rather it is qualification in a person for worship, i.e., prayer. So what you objected is repelled.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi with its commentary explains two key objection methods in qiyas: naqd (refutation by showing inconsistency of the 'illa) and mu'arada (opposition by providing a counter-'illa). The author first uses the example of Islam and apostasy. Someone argues that Islam should be a cause for dissolving marriage just like apostasy, because they are opposites. The Hanafi response is that Islam is a protecting covenant (ʿahd ʿāṣim) that safeguards ownership and rights, so it cannot be an effective cause for removal. This illustrates naqd: the 'illa (Islam) exists in many cases where the ruling (dissolution) does not apply. Then the example of wudu and niyyah is given: the opponent claims that wudu requires niyyah because it is a purification act like tayammum, and both are worship. The Hanafi refutation is by naqd: washing clothes and vessels is also purification but does not require niyyah. The Hanafis then distinguish between haqiqiyya (physical) purification and hukmiyya (legal) purification. Wudu's 'illa is legal purification, which is not rationally comprehensible and thus needs niyyah, whereas physical purification does not. A further discussion compares wiping (masah) in wudu with washing (ghusl). Masah is a pillar but its threefold repetition is not sunnah, unlike washing. This too is presented as an example where the existence of the 'illa (the act being a component of purification) does not carry the ruling of sunnah repetition. The passage also clarifies the difference between tanāquḍ (contradiction) and taʿāruḍ (conflict): contradiction invalidates the evidence itself, while conflict merely prevents the establishment of a ruling without invalidating the evidence. This section is a classic Hanafi usul analysis refining the methodology of qiyas.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): The existence of the claimed 'illa in a case where the ruling is absent, used as an objection against the 'illa.
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): An objection in qiyas by presenting a counter-argument that prevents the establishment of the ruling without necessarily invalidating the original evidence.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause or attribute that is used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case in qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between naqd and mu'arada in Hanafi usul?
A) Naqd invalidates the evidence, while mu'arada only prevents the ruling.
B) Naqd is a form of mu'arada that includes a counter-argument.
C) Naqd deals with the text of the Quran, while mu'arada deals with hadith.
D) They are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Naqd and mu'arada are distinct objection techniques in Hanafi qiyas: naqd attacks the consistency of the 'illa, while mu'arada offers a competing 'illa; the Hanafi response to naqd often involves refining the 'illa to preserve the qiyas.
